Enabling CORS:
--------------
üìå Enabling CORS in Django for File Uploads
When a frontend application (hosted on a different port or domain) attempts to make requests to a Django API, it triggers browser-enforced Cross-Origin Resource Sharing (CORS) restrictions. This step outlines how to configure Django to safely accept such requests, especially for file uploads.




üìå1. Understanding the Problem

‚û§ Scenario:
- Client app is served at: 'http://localhost:8001'
- Django API runs at: 'http://localhost:8000'

Even though both run on the same machine, they are considered different origins due to port mismatch.

‚û§ Result:
The browser blocks requests from the client to the API, showing:
Could not reach the server.





üìå 2. What Is CORS?
CORS (Cross-Origin Resource Sharing) is a browser security mechanism that:
- Prevents unauthorized cross-origin requests
- Ensures APIs only accept requests from approved sources

In our case, we must tell Django to explicitly allow requests from 'localhost:8001'.





üìå 3. Installing Required Library
Use the official package: ['django-cors-headers'](https://pypi.org/project/django-cors-headers/)
pipenv install django-cors-headers




üìå 4.Configuration in 'settings.py'

‚û§ a) Add to Installed Apps:

INSTALLED_APPS = [
    # Django built-ins
    'django.contrib.admin',
    ...

    # Third-party apps
    'corsheaders',

    # Local apps
    'store',
    ...
]


‚û§ b) Add Middleware (must be near the top):
MIDDLEWARE = [
    'corsheaders.middleware.CorsMiddleware',
    'django.middleware.security.SecurityMiddleware',
    ...
]

üîç *CORS middleware must appear before 'CommonMiddleware' to ensure proper processing.*


‚û§ c) Define Allowed Origins:
CORS_ALLOWED_ORIGINS = [
    'http://localhost:8001',
    'http://127.0.0.1:8001',
]


‚úî This allows requests from both domain variants.




üìå 5. Testing the Setup

‚û§ Initial Behavior:
- Uploading a file from client app: Fails with CORS error

‚û§ After Enabling CORS:
- Upload succeeds
- Backend returns an image URL
- Upload progress is visible via the progress bar




üìå 6. Using Chrome DevTools to Inspect Requests

‚úî Go to Developer Tools ‚Üí Network Tab ‚Üí Throttling ‚Üí Slow 3G  
‚úî Upload a file to watch request flow in slow motion

üîç Request Breakdown:
1. OPTIONS request  
   - Used for preflight check
   - Server responds with headers:
     '''
     Access-Control-Allow-Origin: http://localhost:8001
     '''
2. POST request  
   - File upload is executed upon CORS approval

‚úî After successful upload, Chrome shows both requests sequentially.





üìå 7. Restoring Default Network Mode

‚úî Don‚Äôt forget to disable throttling after testing:
- DevTools ‚Üí Network tab ‚Üí Throttling ‚Üí No throttling




Codes and Other Notes in this discussion: 

django cors headers --> https://pypi.org/project/django-cors-headers/


in settings.py:

CORS_ALLOWED_ORIGINS = [
    'http://localhost:8001',
    'http://127.0.0.1:8001',
]





üìå Key Takeaways
‚úî CORS prevents clients from different origins from accessing APIs by default.  
‚úî Use 'django-cors-headers' to allow trusted frontend domains to send requests.  
‚úî Register the middleware early in the list.  
‚úî Use 'CORS_ALLOWED_ORIGINS' to explicitly whitelist client domains.  
‚úî Preflight 'OPTIONS' requests are automatically handled by the middleware.  
‚úî Use browser dev tools to confirm CORS headers and troubleshoot file uploads.



























Managing Images in the Admin:
-----------------------------
üìå Managing Product Images in the Django Admin Interface
This section covers the full implementation of managing and previewing product images inside the Django admin using inline editing and thumbnail previews.




üìå 1. Displaying Product Images Inline

‚û§ Context:
By default, the Product admin page doesn't show associated images.  
We want to manage images just like we do with tags‚Äîusing inlines.

‚û§ Refresher: Admin Layers
| Admin Class Location | Responsibility                                                              |
|----------------------|-----------------------------------------------------------------------------|
| 'store/admin.py'     | Reusable logic specific to the store app (e.g. 'ProductAdmin')              |
| 'core/admin.py'      | Project-specific logic that extends 'ProductAdmin' via 'CustomProductAdmin' |




üìå 2. Defining an Inline for ProductImage

üìÑ store/admin.py:
class ProductImageInline(admin.TabularInline):
    model = models.ProductImage
    readonly_fields = ['thumbnail']

    def thumbnail(self, instance):
        if instance.image.name != '':
            return format_html(f'<img src="{instance.image.url}" class="thumbnail" />')
        return ''


‚úî 'thumbnail()' renders the image as an HTML tag  
‚úî 'readonly_fields' registers it in the UI  
‚úî 'format_html()' is used to safely inject HTML  




üìå 3. Registering the Inline in Both Admin Classes

üìÑ In 'store/admin.py' ‚Üí ProductAdmin:

@admin.register(models.Product)
class ProductAdmin(admin.ModelAdmin):
    ...
    inlines = [ProductImageInline]



üìÑ In 'core/admin.py' ‚Üí CustomProductAdmin

class CustomProductAdmin(ProductAdmin):
    inlines = [TagInline, ProductImageInline]

‚úî Django combines reusable logic from the 'store' app with project-specific enhancements in 'core'.





üìå 4. Styling the Image as a Thumbnail

‚û§ Define a CSS class:
üìÅ 'store/static/store/styles.css'

'''css
.thumbnail {
  width: 100px;
  height: 100px;
  object-fit: cover;
}
'''

‚úî 'width' & 'height': Forces a square frame  
‚úî 'object-fit: cover': Prevents image distortion





üìå 5. Loading the CSS in Admin via Media Class

üìÑ Inside ProductAdmin:
class ProductAdmin(admin.ModelAdmin):
    ...

    class Media:
        css = {
            'all': ['store/styles.css']
        }


‚úî This loads 'styles.css' on all product admin views  
‚úî ''all'' is a CSS media type (applies to screen, print, etc.)





üìå 6. Namespacing Static Files to Avoid Conflicts

‚úî By default, Django collects all static files from installed apps  
‚úî If two apps have a file named 'styles.css', one could overwrite the other

üìÅ Organize like this:

store/
‚îî‚îÄ‚îÄ static/
    ‚îî‚îÄ‚îÄ store/
        ‚îî‚îÄ‚îÄ styles.css


‚úî Update the 'Media' class to use: ''store/styles.css''






Codes and Other Notes in this Discussion: 


core/admin.py:

class TagInline(GenericTabularInline):
    autocomplete_fields = ['tag']
    model = TaggedItem


class CustomProductAdmin(ProductAdmin):
    inlines = [TagInline, ProductImageInline]


admin.site.unregister(Product)
admin.site.register(Product, CustomProductAdmin)




store/admin.py:
from django.utils.html import format_html, urlencode

class ProductImageInline(admin.TabularInline):
    model = models.ProductImage
    readonly_fields = ['thumbnail']

    def thumbnail(self, instance):
        if instance.image.name != '':
            return format_html(f'<img src="{instance.image.url}" class="thumbnail" />')
        return ''


@admin.register(models.Product)
class ProductAdmin(admin.ModelAdmin):
    autocomplete_fields = ['collection']
    prepopulated_fields = {
        'slug': ['title']
    }
    actions = ['clear_inventory']
    inlines = [ProductImageInline]
    list_display = ['title', 'unit_price',
                    'inventory_status', 'collection_title']
    list_editable = ['unit_price']
    list_filter = ['collection', 'last_update', InventoryFilter]
    list_per_page = 10
    list_select_related = ['collection']
    search_fields = ['title']

    def collection_title(self, product):
        return product.collection.title

    @admin.display(ordering='inventory')
    def inventory_status(self, product):
        if product.inventory < 10:
            return 'Low'
        return 'OK'

    @admin.action(description='Clear inventory')
    def clear_inventory(self, request, queryset):
        updated_count = queryset.update(inventory=0)
        self.message_user(
            request,
            f'{updated_count} products were successfully updated.',
            messages.ERROR
        )

    class Media:
        css = {
            'all': ['store/styles.css']
        }
		


store\static\store\styles.css:
		
.thumbnail {
  width: 100px;
  height: 100px;
  object-fit: cover;
}




üìå Key Takeaways

‚úî Define a 'TabularInline' for displaying related 'ProductImage' entries.  
‚úî Render thumbnails via a method using 'format_html()' and 'instance.image.url'.  
‚úî Apply custom CSS to style images as thumbnails.  
‚úî Use 'Media' class to load static assets in the admin.  
‚úî Namespace static files inside 'static/store/' to avoid conflicts across apps.















Sending Emails:
---------------
In this section, we'll be talking about sending emails from your Django applications. 



Setting up a Fake SMTP Server:
------------------------------
üìß Setting Up a Fake SMTP Server for Email Testing
In order to simulate email sending in a development environment, we need a local SMTP server. Instead of using a real (often paid) SMTP provider during development, we'll set up a fake SMTP server using SMTP4DEV, a powerful testing tool.



üìå 1. What Is SMTP?
- SMTP stands for *Simple Mail Transfer Protocol*.
- It is a protocol used to send and receive emails.
- Real SMTP servers (e.g., Gmail, SendGrid) are used in production, and usually require authentication and sometimes cost money.





üìå 2. Why Use a Fake SMTP Server in Development?
‚úî Avoids sending real emails  
‚úî Lets developers preview emails sent by the application  
‚úî Offers an admin panel to visualize email content




üìå 3. Introducing SMTP4DEV

GitHub link: [https://github.com/rnwood/smtp4dev](https://github.com/rnwood/smtp4dev)
SMTP4DEV is a fake SMTP server designed for developers. It captures sent emails and displays them in a web-based interface.




üìå 4. Installation Options

You can install SMTP4DEV in one of two ways:

‚û§ Option 1: Using .NET Core (v3.1+)
dotnet tool install Rnwood.Smtp4dev --global
smtp4dev

‚úî Recommended for systems that already use .NET SDK.



‚û§ Option 2: Using Docker (Preferred)
Docker is a container platform used to run applications in isolated environments.

üìå If Docker is not installed:
- Visit [https://www.docker.com/products/docker-desktop](https://www.docker.com/products/docker-desktop)
- Download and install Docker Desktop for your OS

üìå Then run the following command:
docker run --rm -it -p 3000:80 -p 25:25 rnwood/smtp4dev


‚úî This pulls the SMTP4DEV Docker image from Docker Hub and exposes:
- Port 25 for sending emails
- Port 3000 for the browser-based admin panel





üìå 5. Confirming It Works

‚û§ Access the Admin Panel
- Open your browser and navigate to:
  '''
  http://localhost:3000
  '''

- This panel functions like a mock email inbox.
- Any emails sent by your Django app during development will appear here.



üìå Visual Overview:
[Django App] --(send email)--> [SMTP4DEV @ localhost:25]
                             ‚Ü≥ View messages at http://localhost:3000


‚úî Acts like an inbox (Outlook/Webmail)  
‚úî Helps visualize subject, recipients, headers, HTML content, and attachments



Codes and Other Notes in this discussion:

smtp4dev --> https://github.com/rnwood/smtp4dev



üìå Key Takeaways
‚úî SMTP4DEV helps you test email functionality without sending real emails  
‚úî You can view and debug email content in a browser-friendly admin panel  
‚úî Installation is quick using Docker or .NET CLI  
‚úî This setup is only for local development ‚Äî production uses real SMTP providers



Once set up, you‚Äôre ready to configure your Django project to use this SMTP server. That‚Äôs what we‚Äôll cover next. Let me know if you want a Docker-free setup or help integrating this with Django's 'EMAIL_BACKEND'. üìÆüöÄ

















Configuring the Email Backend: 
------------------------------


üì© Configuring the Email Backend in Django
Django provides multiple email backends for different environments and use cases. This setup explains how to configure Django to send emails via an SMTP server, specifically using a fake SMTP server like smtp4dev in a development environment.



üìå 1. What is an Email Backend?

An email backend in Django is a component responsible for handling the actual sending of emails. Django supports various backends for different scenarios:

| Backend Type | Description |
|--------------|-------------|
| SMTP         | Default backend. Sends emails via an SMTP server. |
| Console      | Prints emails to the terminal instead of sending them (used for local dev/testing). |
| File         | Writes emails to files in a designated directory. |
| LocMem       | Stores emails in memory (typically for testing). |
| Dummy        | Discards all email actions (used for disabling email entirely). |

üìå Reference: ''django.core.mail.backends.smtp.EmailBackend'' is Django‚Äôs default backend.





üìå 2. Configuring the SMTP Backend in 'settings.py'

EMAIL_BACKEND = 'django.core.mail.backends.smtp.EmailBackend'
EMAIL_HOST = 'localhost'             # Address of the SMTP server
EMAIL_HOST_USER = ''                 # Not required for fake SMTP
EMAIL_HOST_PASSWORD = ''            # Not required for fake SMTP
EMAIL_PORT = 2525                   # Default SMTP is 25; smtp4dev uses 2525
DEFAULT_FROM_EMAIL = 'from@moshbuy.com'


‚úÖ Even though the SMTP backend is default, specifying it makes the configuration explicit.  
‚úÖ 'localhost' refers to where our SMTP server (e.g., smtp4dev) is hosted.  
‚úÖ 'EMAIL_PORT = 2525' because smtp4dev maps port 2525 for incoming email requests.





üìå 3. Avoid Hardcoding Credentials in Production

For real SMTP services (SendGrid, Mailgun, etc.):
- You will need to supply valid 'EMAIL_HOST_USER' and 'EMAIL_HOST_PASSWORD'.
- Avoid hardcoding passwords in 'settings.py'.

‚ö†Ô∏è Best Practice:
Use environment variables to securely store credentials:

import os

EMAIL_HOST_USER = os.environ.get('EMAIL_HOST_USER')
EMAIL_HOST_PASSWORD = os.environ.get('EMAIL_HOST_PASSWORD')

You can load these using '.env' files and libraries like 'python-decouple' or 'django-environ'.




‚úÖ Final Sample Snippet for Development

EMAIL_BACKEND = 'django.core.mail.backends.smtp.EmailBackend'
EMAIL_HOST = 'localhost'
EMAIL_PORT = 2525
EMAIL_HOST_USER = ''
EMAIL_HOST_PASSWORD = ''
DEFAULT_FROM_EMAIL = 'from@moshbuy.com'






Codes and Other Notes in this discussion: 

Backends: 
SMTP(Default)
Console
File
Locmem
Dummy


settings.py:
EMAIL_BACKEND = 'django.core.mail.backends.smtp.EmailBackend'
EMAIL_HOST = 'localhost'
EMAIL_HOST_USER = ''
EMAIL_HOST_PASSWORD = ''
EMAIL_PORT = 2525   #by default smtp servers runs on port 25
DEFAULT_FROM_EMAIL = 'from@moshbuy.com'





üìå Key Takeaways
‚úî Django provides several email backends; SMTP is the default.  
‚úî Use 'smtp4dev' during development for safe and local email testing.  
‚úî Set 'EMAIL_BACKEND', 'EMAIL_HOST', and 'EMAIL_PORT' to connect to your SMTP server.  
‚úî In real applications, use environment variables to protect credentials.  
‚úî You can switch to the console backend for quick debugging:
EMAIL_BACKEND = 'django.core.mail.backends.console.EmailBackend'


























Sending Emails:
---------------
üìß Sending Emails in Django
With the email backend and SMTP server (like smtp4dev) configured, Django can now send emails using its built-in utilities. This note covers how to send emails to users and site admins with support for plain text and HTML content, and how to handle common errors safely.



üìå 1. Django‚Äôs Built-in Email Utilities

Import these tools from 'django.core.mail':

from django.core.mail import send_mail, mail_admins, BadHeaderError


Available Functions:

| Function          | Purpose                                   |
|-------------------|-------------------------------------------|
| 'send_mail()'     | Sends a single email                      |
| 'send_mass_mail()'| Sends multiple emails over one connection |
| 'mail_admins()'   | Sends email to site admins (from settings)|
| 'mail_managers()' | Sends email to site managers              |

‚ö†Ô∏è 'send_mail()' opens a new connection per email;  
'send_mass_mail()' reuses a single connection ‚Äî better for bulk sending.





üìå 2. Sending a Basic Email:

Example: 'playground/views.py'

def say_hello(request):
    try:
        send_mail(
            subject='subject',
            message='message',
            from_email='info@moshbuy.com',        # overrides DEFAULT_FROM_EMAIL
            recipient_list=['bob@moshbuy.com']
        )
    except BadHeaderError:
        pass  # should ideally return an error response
    return render(request, 'hello.html', {'name': 'Mosh'})


üîç Notes:
- 'BadHeaderError' is raised if malicious users inject headers (e.g., spoofing).
- Always catch and handle this exception securely.





üìå 3. Verifying in smtp4dev (Port 3000)

After triggering this view (e.g., visiting '/playground/hello'), smtp4dev will display the sent email:
- View subject, recipient, and body
- Confirm delivery without actually sending to external servers





üìå 4. Sending Email to Site Admins

Django can email site admins using 'mail_admins()'.

def say_hello(request):
    try:
        mail_admins(
            subject='subject',
            message='message',
            html_message='<strong>message</strong>'
        )
    except BadHeaderError:
        pass
    return render(request, 'hello.html', {'name': 'Mosh'})


üß© Configuration in 'settings.py'

ADMINS = [
    ('Mosh', 'admin@moshbuy.com')
]

'mail_admins()' ignores the 'User' model ‚Äî it relies solely on the 'ADMINS' setting.





üìå 5. Plain Text vs HTML Emails

- 'message' ‚Üí plain text content (suitable for all mail clients)
- 'html_message' ‚Üí optional HTML version of the email

üîç Why Send Both?
Some clients (e.g., screen readers or legacy devices) may only support plain text. Including both ensures maximum compatibility.

üí° smtp4dev Insight:
In the "Parts" tab of the email preview:
- You‚Äôll see both 'text/plain' and 'text/html' versions.
- Clients will render the preferred format based on support.






Codes and Other Notes in this Discussion: 

playground/views.py:

from django.core.mail import send_mail, mail_admins, BadHeaderError 

def say_hello(request):
	try:
		send_mail('subject', 'message', 'info@moshbuy.com', ['bob@moshbuy.com'])
	except BadHeaderError:
		pass
	return render(request, 'hello.html', {'name': 'Mosh'})
	
	
#to mail_admins
def say_hello(request):
	try:
		mail_admins('subject', 'message', html_message='message')
	except BadHeaderError:
		pass
	return render(request, 'hello.html', {'name': 'Mosh'})
	
in settings.py:
ADMINS = [
    ('Mosh', 'admin@moshbuy.com')
]
	
	
	
	
	
‚úÖ Summary

| Task                        | Description                                                  |
|-----------------------------|--------------------------------------------------------------|
| 'send_mail()'               | Sends a user-facing email                                    |
| 'mail_admins()'             | Sends site-wide alerts to configured admins                  |
| 'BadHeaderError'            | Protects against header injection attacks                    |
| 'html_message'              | Sends a formatted HTML version along with plain text         |
| 'ADMINS' in settings        | Registers recipients for admin-level notifications           |


















Attaching Files:
----------------


üìé Attaching Files in Django Emails
By default, Django provides utility functions like 'send_mail()' and 'mail_admins()' for basic email functionality. However, if you want finer control over email content‚Äîsuch as adding attachments, CC/BCC support, or setting specific headers‚Äîyou should use the 'EmailMessage' class directly.



üìå 1. Why Use 'EmailMessage'?
Internally, helper functions like 'send_mail()' use 'EmailMessage' behind the scenes. But by constructing an 'EmailMessage' yourself, you unlock additional features like:
- File attachments
- Multiple recipients
- CC/BCC support
- Inline image support (advanced use cases)



üìå 2. Attaching a File to an Email
‚úîÔ∏è Step-by-Step Setup
üß± File Structure (Relative to Project Root):

playground/
‚îî‚îÄ‚îÄ static/
    ‚îî‚îÄ‚îÄ images/
        ‚îî‚îÄ‚îÄ dog.jpg


You can drag-and-drop your image into this folder to simulate attaching a file.



üßæ Sample Implementation
üìÑ File: 'playground/views.py':
from django.core.mail import EmailMessage, BadHeaderError
from django.shortcuts import render

def say_hello(request):
    try:
        message = EmailMessage(
            subject='subject',
            body='message',
            from_email='from@moshbuy.com',
            to=['bob@moshbuy.com']
        )
        message.attach_file('playground/static/images/dog.jpg')
        message.send()
    except BadHeaderError:
        pass  # In production, log and return proper error response

    return render(request, 'hello.html', {'name': 'Mosh'})





üìå 3. Explanation of Key Elements

| Parameter          | Description                                          |
|--------------------|------------------------------------------------------|
| 'subject'          | Email subject line                                   |
| 'body'             | Body of the message (can be plain text or HTML)     |
| 'from_email'       | Sender‚Äôs email address                               |
| 'to'               | List of recipient email addresses                    |
| 'attach_file(path)'| Attaches the file from the given path                |

‚ö†Ô∏è The file path is relative to the project root.



‚úÖ Result

After triggering the view (e.g., reloading '/playground/hello/'), smtp4dev will show:
- An email with the subject line and body message
- A file attachment: 'dog.jpg'
- You can preview and download the attached file in the admin UI





Codes and Other Notes in this discussion: 

from django.core.mail import EmailMessage, BadHeaderError
from django.shortcuts import render


def say_hello(request):
    try:
       message = EmailMessage('subject', 'message', 'from@moshbuy.com', ['bob@moshbuy.com']
       message.attach_file('playground/static/images/dog.jpg')
	   message.send()
    except BadHeaderError:
        pass
    return render(request, 'hello.html', {'name': 'Mosh'})
	
	
		

üìå Key Takeaways

‚úî Use 'EmailMessage' when you need more control than 'send_mail()' provides  
‚úî Attach files using '.attach_file(path)' with correct file path  
‚úî Always wrap 'send()' in a 'try-except' block to catch 'BadHeaderError'  
‚úî Use smtp4dev to test your email setup without sending real emails  
‚úî Useful for order receipts, invoices, downloadable reports, or any file delivery use case

	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
Sending Templated Emails:
-------------------------	
üì© Sending Templated Emails in Django
Hardcoding email messages directly in your views can quickly become unmanageable‚Äîespecially when emails grow in length or need to support dynamic content. Instead, we can use the 'django-templated-mail' library to manage email messages through reusable templates.



üìå 1. Install 'django-templated-mail'

pipenv install django-templated-mail

This package provides the 'BaseEmailMessage' class‚Äîan extension of Django‚Äôs built-in 'EmailMessage'‚Äîwith support for rendering subject, plain text, and HTML bodies via Django templates.



üìå 2. Create a Templated Email:
üìÅ File: 'playground/templates/emails/hello.html'

{% block subject %}This is a long subject{% endblock %}

{% block html_body %}
<h1>Hello</h1>
My name is {{ name }}
{% endblock %}


üîç Explanation:
- '{% block subject %}' ‚Üí Defines the email subject
- '{% block html_body %}' ‚Üí Contains HTML-formatted message content
- '{{ name }}' ‚Üí Dynamically rendered using a context dictionary

‚úî You may also define a '{% block text_body %}' for plain-text clients (optional).

> ‚ö†Ô∏è To avoid formatting issues in VS Code when editing block templates, use Auto Save and avoid manual saving (which may reformat blocks poorly).



üìå 3. Using 'BaseEmailMessage' in Views

üìÑ File: 'playground/views.py':
from django.shortcuts import render
from templated_mail.mail import BaseEmailMessage
from django.core.mail import BadHeaderError

def say_hello(request):
    try:
        message = BaseEmailMessage(
            template_name='emails/hello.html',
            context={'name': 'Mosh'}
        )
        message.send(['john@moshbuy.com'])
    except BadHeaderError:
        pass
    return render(request, 'hello.html', {'name': 'Mosh'})


üîç Key Points:
| Parameter        | Purpose                                               |
|------------------|--------------------------------------------------------|
| 'template_name'  | Relative path to the email template                    |
| 'context'        | Dictionary of variables to be rendered in the template |
| '.send(to=[])'   | Required list of recipient emails                      |

‚úî 'BaseEmailMessage.send()' expects the 'to' parameter to be passed explicitly.



‚úÖ Final Output (via smtp4dev):

When triggered:
- The recipient receives an email with the subject:  
  _"This is a long subject"_
- The body renders:
  '''html
  <h1>Hello</h1>
  My name is Mosh
  '''

‚úî You can preview both HTML and plain text sections in the 'Parts' tab of smtp4dev.





Codes and Other Notes in this Discussion: 	
		
pipenv install django-templated-mail	

playground\templates\emails\hello.html:
{% block subject %}This is a long subject{% endblock %}

{% block html_body %}
<h1>Hello</h1>
My name is {{ name }}
{% endblock %}


views.py:	
from django.core.mail import EmailMessage, BadHeaderError
from django.shortcuts import render
from templated_mail.mail import BaseEmailMessage


def say_hello(request):
    try:
       message = BaseEmailMessage(
           template_name='emails/hello.html',
           context={'name': 'Mosh'}
       )
       message.send(['john@moshbuy.com'])
    except BadHeaderError:
        pass
    return render(request, 'hello.html', {'name': 'Mosh'})
	
	
	
	
	
üìå Key Takeaways
‚úî Use 'django-templated-mail' to cleanly separate email content from Python logic.  
‚úî Email content is stored and rendered from template files.  
‚úî Templates support plain text and HTML blocks‚Äîalong with dynamic variables.  
‚úî Use 'BaseEmailMessage' with 'template_name' and 'context' to generate and send the email.  
‚úî Always namespace your templates (e.g., 'emails/hello.html') to avoid collisions.




















Running Background Tasks:
-------------------------

In this section, we'll be talking about running background tasks in Django applications. 


Introduction to Celery:
-----------------------
üìå Introduction to Celery ‚Äî Running Background Tasks in Django

Modern web applications often deal with resource-intensive tasks like:
- Sending emails
- Processing uploaded files (images, videos)
- Generating reports
- Running machine learning models
- Importing/exporting large data sets

These operations are typically slow and CPU-heavy, and should not block the request-response cycle of the main application. For a responsive and scalable system, such tasks should be offloaded to the background.



üîÑ Real-World Scenario
Let‚Äôs say a user uploads a video to your app.  
‚ùå If the backend processes the video in the same request cycle, the user experiences long wait times.  

‚úÖ Instead, we send the video to a background worker, and respond immediately:  
‚ÄúWe‚Äôre processing your video. You‚Äôll be notified when it‚Äôs ready.‚Äù


üß† Why Offload Background Tasks?

| Problem                         | Background Solution                     |
|---------------------------------|-----------------------------------------|
| Long-running tasks block server | Offload work to background worker       |
| Risk of request timeouts        | Reduce load on main process             |
| Poor user experience            | Immediate response improves UX          |
| Inflexible scaling              | Easily add workers to process more jobs |



üöÄ Meet Celery
Celery is a popular Python framework for executing asynchronous tasks and scheduling periodic jobs.  
Website: [https://docs.celeryq.dev](https://docs.celeryq.dev)


üåü Key Benefits:
- Offloads long-running tasks from the Django app
- Manages distributed task queues
- Supports scheduling (e.g., run every Monday 9 AM)
- Scales easily with multiple worker processes
- Is fault-tolerant: if a task fails, the Django app isn‚Äôt impacted



‚öôÔ∏è How Celery Works (Conceptual Flow):
[Django App] ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚ñ∂ [Task Queue] ‚óÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚ñ∂ [Celery Workers]
       ‚îÇ                               ‚ñ≤
       ‚îî‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ Immediate Response ‚îÇ


1. App pushes a task to the queue (e.g., send email).
2. Celery worker picks up the task.
3. Worker processes the task and marks it complete.
4. Django remains responsive to other users.


üïí Bonus: Task Scheduling
Celery can run periodic (cron-like) jobs:
- Run cleanup every day at midnight
- Rebuild cache every 6 hours
- Send weekly reports

This is done using Celery Beat (a scheduler that kicks off tasks based on a defined interval).




Codes and Other Notes in this Discussion: 

Long-Running Tasks:
Processing images and videos
Generating reports 
Sending emails
Running machine learning models 



üìå Key Takeaways:
‚úî Celery enables running tasks asynchronously, outside the main Django process  
‚úî Great for heavy, blocking, or scheduled tasks  
‚úî Frees up Django to continue handling user requests instantly  
‚úî Makes your app more responsive, scalable, and robust














Message Brokers:
----------------
üìå Understanding Message Brokers in Celery
In Celery-based architecture, tasks are sent to a queue by the main application and picked up by Celery workers for background execution. The message broker serves as the middleman between the application and the workers.


üìå 1. What Is a Message Broker?
A message broker is software that:
- Acts as a queueing system for message delivery.
- Ensures reliable communication between producers (e.g., Django app) and consumers (e.g., Celery workers).
- Stores messages temporarily until they can be processed.

Think of it like a pipe that holds tasks and delivers them to available workers in order.


üìå 2. Real-World Analogy
In English, the word "broker" means a middleman. For example:
- If you want insurance, instead of contacting every company yourself, you talk to an insurance broker.
- The broker connects you with the best option based on your needs.

In the same way, a message broker connects sender and receiver systems ‚Äî even if one of them is offline ‚Äî and guarantees message delivery.



üìå 3. Message Flow Architecture:

[Django App] ‚îÄ‚îÄ Send Task ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚ñ∂ [Message Broker (Queue)] ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚ñ∂ [Celery Worker]

- The Django app creates a task and sends it to the message broker.
- The broker queues the task for the next available Celery worker.
- If the worker is offline, the broker stores the task and retries later.

‚úî If the message broker fails, you can configure a cluster of brokers for fault tolerance.



üìå 4. Popular Message Brokers

| Broker     | Type                         | Notes                                                   | 
|------------|------------------------------|---------------------------------------------------------|
| Redis      | In-memory data store         | Simple, lightweight, easy to set up                     |
| RabbitMQ   | Full-featured message broker | Enterprise-grade, supports complex routing, more robust |

‚úî Redis is not originally a broker‚Äîbut supports broker functionality.  
‚úî RabbitMQ is a dedicated broker with greater reliability and advanced features‚Äîbut more complex to manage.




üìå 5. Which One Should You Use?

For most Django projects:
- Start with Redis: Fast, minimal setup, works well for most use cases.
- Move to RabbitMQ only if Redis no longer meets your needs (e.g., delayed messaging, guaranteed ordering, or advanced routing).

üîÅ Flexibility: Celery abstracts the broker behind an interface, so you can easily switch from Redis to RabbitMQ with minimal changes.




Codes and Other Notes in this Discussion: 

Brokers:
Redis(in-memory data store)
RabbitMQ(real, enterprise-grade broker)



‚úÖ Summary

‚úî A message broker is essential for offloading tasks from Django to Celery workers.  
‚úî It guarantees message delivery even if the recipient (worker) is temporarily unavailable.  
‚úî Redis is an excellent starting point‚Äîsimple and versatile.  
‚úî RabbitMQ provides enterprise-grade control but adds complexity.  
‚úî You can cluster brokers for high availability.














Installing Redis: 
-----------------
üì¶ Installing Redis with Docker for Django Projects:
To enable background task processing with Celery, we need a message broker, and in this course we‚Äôll be using Redis. The fastest and most convenient way to install Redis locally is via Docker, a lightweight containerization platform.


üìå 1. What Is Redis?
- Redis is an in-memory data store that can be used as:
  - A database
  - A cache
  - A message broker for Celery tasks



üìå 2. Why Use Docker for Redis?
‚úî Running Redis in Docker:
- Avoids manual installation on your OS
- Keeps Redis isolated from the host system
- Easily configurable with port mapping
- Lightweight and restartable

üí° Think of Docker containers as lightweight virtual machines‚Äîfully isolated and disposable environments.



üìå 3. Running Redis in Detached Mode
Open your terminal and run:
docker run -d -p 6379:6379 redis

üîç Command Breakdown:
| Part               | Description                                      |
|--------------------|--------------------------------------------------|
| 'docker run'       | Launch a new container                           |
| '-d'               | Run in detached mode (in the background)         |
| '-p 6379:6379'     | Map port 6379 on localhost to container          |
| 'redis'            | Official Docker image for Redis (downloaded from Docker Hub) |

‚úî Port 6379 is Redis's default port for accepting connections.



üìå 4. Verifying the Redis Container
To confirm Redis is running:
docker ps

You should see output similar to:
CONTAINER ID   IMAGE     PORTS                    STATUS
123abc456def   redis     0.0.0.0:6379->6379/tcp   Up 2 minutes

‚úî This confirms that:
- Redis is running inside a container
- Port '6379' on your machine is forwarded to port '6379' in the container



üìå 5. Installing the Redis Python Package
For Django to communicate with Redis, install the 'redis' Python client in your Pipenv environment:
pipenv install redis

‚úî This will allow libraries like Celery to connect to Redis via Python.

üìå Visual Summary:

[Your Django Project] ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚ñ∂ Redis (localhost:6379)
        ‚ñ≤                            ‚ñ≤
        ‚îÇ   pipenv install redis     ‚îÇ
        ‚îî‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ docker run -p 6379 ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îÄ‚îò


Redis now acts as a message queue that will later be consumed by Celery workers.



Code and Other Notes in this Discussion: 

docker run -d -p 6379:6379 redis 

docker ps

pipenv install redis 



‚úÖ Key Takeaways:
‚úî Redis is essential as a message broker for Celery in Django.  
‚úî Docker provides a clean and quick way to run Redis without installing it natively.  
‚úî Use '-p 6379:6379' to expose Redis on the expected port.  
‚úî Use 'docker ps' to confirm Redis is running.  
‚úî Install the Python 'redis' package to enable Django/Celery communication.











Celery and Windows:
-------------------
üìå Using Celery on Windows with WSL
Celery no longer supports Windows natively since version 4. This means you cannot run Celery workers directly on a standard Windows environment.


üß≠ Why Celery Doesn‚Äôt Work on Windows
‚úî Celery depends heavily on UNIX-based multiprocessing internals and signal handling, which are not fully supported by Windows OS.  
‚úî Therefore, the recommended solution is to run Django and Celery inside a Linux shell, even on Windows machines.



üñ•Ô∏è Solution: Windows Subsystem for Linux (WSL)
‚û§ What is WSL?
- WSL stands for Windows Subsystem for Linux
- It allows you to run a complete Linux distribution alongside your Windows OS ‚Äîno need for dual boot or full virtualization.
- Provides access to a separate Linux file system, Bash shell, and package manager ('apt', 'pip', etc.)

üí° Think of it as a lightweight Ubuntu shell running side-by-side with Windows apps.



‚öôÔ∏è Benefits of Using WSL:
| Benefit                    | Description                                      |
|----------------------------|--------------------------------------------------|
| Native Linux support       | Run tools like Celery without compatibility issues |
| Separate environment       | Doesn‚Äôt interfere with your Windows system       |
| Access to Linux utilities  | Use Bash, 'apt', 'systemd', etc.                 |
| Persistent environment     | Installed packages stay even after reboot        |



üì• Setup Requirements
‚úî You‚Äôll need:
- WSL 2 installed
- A Linux distribution (e.g., Ubuntu) from the Microsoft Store
- Docker Desktop (if using Celery with Docker)
- Your Django project cloned or moved into the Linux file system



üóÇÔ∏è Workflow Summary

Windows (host)
  ‚îî‚îÄ‚îÄ WSL (Linux shell)
        ‚îú‚îÄ‚îÄ Django App
        ‚îú‚îÄ‚îÄ Celery Worker
        ‚îî‚îÄ‚îÄ Redis Server or Docker-based broker



‚úî You‚Äôll open WSL (e.g., 'Ubuntu') and run commands like:
pipenv shell
celery -A storefront worker -l info
python manage.py runserver



üìå Important Notes:
- Setting up WSL with Django may take 30 minutes to an hour the first time.
- A detailed setup guide (PDF) is typically provided to walk you through steps like:
  - Installing WSL via PowerShell
  - Installing Ubuntu from the Microsoft Store
  - Installing Python, pipenv, and dependencies inside WSL
  - Running Django & Celery services from within Linux



‚úÖ Key Takeaways

‚úî Celery dropped native Windows support‚Äîuse WSL to run it on a Windows machine  
‚úî WSL provides a fully functional Linux environment inside Windows  
‚úî The process requires one-time setup but ensures compatibility and stability  
‚úî Once set up, you‚Äôll be running Django + Celery exactly as on Linux or macOS





















Setting Up Celery:
------------------
üì¶ Setting Up Celery in a Django Project
To execute background tasks asynchronously, we integrate Celery into our Django application. Celery connects to Redis (our message broker) and runs in a separate process that listens for task requests from the main Django app.



üìå 1. Installing Celery
In your Django environment, install Celery:
pipenv install celery



üìå 2. Creating the Celery Configuration Module
üìÑ File: 'storefront/celery.py'

import os
from celery import Celery

os.environ.setdefault('DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE', 'storefront.settings')

celery = Celery('storefront')
celery.config_from_object('django.conf:settings', namespace='CELERY')
celery.autodiscover_tasks()

üîç Explanation:
- 'os.environ.setdefault(...)' ensures Celery knows which settings module to load.
- 'Celery('storefront')' assigns the app a name (used for logging).
- 'config_from_object(...)' loads Celery settings from Django‚Äôs settings module.
- 'namespace='CELERY'' means all Celery config variables in settings must start with 'CELERY_'.
- 'autodiscover_tasks()' allows Celery to detect tasks defined in 'tasks.py' within installed apps.



üìå 3. Configuring Redis as the Message Broker
üìÑ File: 'storefront/settings.py'
CELERY_BROKER_URL = 'redis://localhost:6379/1'

‚úî This URL points to Redis running locally on port '6379', using DB #1.  
‚úî Redis must already be running (e.g., via Docker).




üìå 4. Registering the Celery App
üìÑ File: 'storefront/__init__.py'
from .celery import celery

‚úî This ensures the Celery app gets loaded when Django starts.





üìå 5. Starting the Celery Worker
In a separate terminal window, run the Celery worker:
celery -A storefront worker --loglevel=info

üîç Flags:
- '-A storefront' ‚Üí Points to the Django project where the Celery app is defined.
- 'worker' ‚Üí Starts a worker process to handle tasks.
- '--loglevel=info' ‚Üí Outputs detailed log messages.

‚úÖ Expected Output:
- 'celery@hostname ready' ‚Üí Indicates the worker is successfully connected.
- 'transport=redis' ‚Üí Confirms Redis is being used as the broker.
- 'concurrency=8' ‚Üí Number of worker threads (based on CPU cores).


‚ö†Ô∏è You may see a warning about 'settings.DEBUG' causing memory leaks ‚Äî this is harmless in development.


üìÅ Terminal Sessions Required to Run the Project

| Terminal | Command                                      | Purpose                    |
|----------|----------------------------------------------|----------------------------|
| 1        | 'python manage.py runserver'                 | Start Django development server |
| 2        | 'docker run -p 6379:6379 redis'              | Run Redis in a container        |
| 3        | 'celery -A storefront worker --loglevel=info'| Start Celery worker             |

üí° We‚Äôll later replace this manual process with a single Docker command using Docker Compose.





Code and Other Notes in this Discussion: 

pipenv install celery

storefront/celery.py:

import os 
from celery import Celery

os.environ.setdefault('DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE', 'storefront.settings')

celery = Celery('storefront')
celery.config_from_object('django.conf:settings', namespace='CELERY')
celery.autodiscover_tasks()



storefront/settings.py:
CELERY_BROKER_URL = 'redis://localhost:6379/1'



storefront/__init__.py:
from .celery import celery



celery -A storefront worker --loglevel=info 


Three terminal windows: 
python manage.py runserver #for running webserver 
docker run -p 6379:6379 redis #for running redis
celery -A storefront worker # for running celery 





‚úÖ Key Takeaways

‚úî Install Celery and create a 'celery.py' module inside your Django project.  
‚úî Define Redis as the broker in your Django 'settings.py'.  
‚úî Register the Celery app in '__init__.py' to ensure it loads on startup.  
‚úî Launch Celery with the 'worker' command and verify Redis connectivity.  
‚úî You'll need three terminal sessions (Django server, Redis, Celery worker) during development.















Creating and Executing Tasks: 
-----------------------------
üìå Creating and Executing Background Tasks with Celery in Django
Now that Celery is installed and configured, we can start defining tasks that execute asynchronously in the background. This allows long-running operations to be handled outside the main web server process, improving performance and user experience.



üìå 1. Defining a Celery Task
üìÑ File: 'playground/tasks.py'
from time import sleep
from celery import shared_task  # ‚úÖ Better, reusable pattern

@shared_task
def notify_customers(message):
    print("Sending 10k emails...")
    print(message)
    sleep(10)  # Simulates a long-running process
    print("Emails were successfully sent!")


üîç Why use '@shared_task'?
- ‚úÖ Avoids direct dependency on the project‚Äôs 'celery.py' module.
- ‚úÖ Keeps your app reusable and independent.
- üö´ Avoid using 'from storefront.celery import celery' with '@celery.task' if your app is intended to be reusable.




üìå 2. Executing the Task from a View
üìÑ File: 'playground/views.py':
from django.shortcuts import render
from .tasks import notify_customers

def say_hello(request):
    notify_customers.delay('Hello')  # ‚ö†Ô∏è Use '.delay()' to queue the task!
    return render(request, 'hello.html', {'name': 'Mosh'})


üìå Note:
- Don‚Äôt call 'notify_customers('Hello')' directly‚Äîthat would run it immediately.
- '.delay()' sends the task to the Celery worker via Redis, allowing it to run in the background.






üìå 3. Auto-Discovery of Tasks

Recall from the Celery setup:
celery.autodiscover_tasks()

‚úî This ensures Celery automatically picks up all 'tasks.py' modules in installed Django apps.





üìå 4. Running and Debugging Tasks

‚ùó Common Issue:
When Celery is already running and you define a new task:
Received unregistered task of type 'playground.tasks.notify_customers'


‚úÖ Solution:
- Stop and restart the worker:
ctrl + C
celery -A storefront worker --loglevel=info


Now Celery loads the updated code and registers the new task.





üìå 5. Background Execution with Reliability

- When 'notify_customers.delay()' is called, the task runs in the background.
- The Django view returns immediately (even though the task sleeps for 10 seconds).
- You can monitor the logs in the Celery worker terminal.






üìå 6. Message Broker Guarantees Delivery

Even if the Celery worker is offline:
- Redis stores the message.
- When the worker restarts, Redis retries and delivers the task.

üîç Example Workflow:
[User Hits /hello/] ‚îÄ‚ñ∂ Django App ‚îÄ‚ñ∂ Redis Queue ‚îÄ‚ñ∂ (Celery Worker is Offline)
                                               ‚ñ≤
                                   [Retry When Worker is Online]

‚úî This demonstrates asynchronous execution and guaranteed task delivery, key advantages of using a message broker like Redis.





Codes and Other Notes in this Discussion: 

playground/tasks.py:

from time import sleep
from storefront.celery import celery


@celery.task
def notify_customers(message):
    print('Sending 10k emails...')
    print(message)
    sleep(10)
    print('Emails were successfully sent!')





Better way: 
playground/tasks.py:

from time import sleep
from celery import shared_task


@shared_task
def notify_customers(message):
    print('Sending 10k emails...')
    print(message)
    sleep(10)
    print('Emails were successfully sent!')



playground/views.py:
from django.shortcuts import render
from .tasks import notify_customers


def say_hello(request):
    notify_customers.delay('Hello')
    return render(request, 'hello.html', {'name': 'Mosh'})




‚úÖ Key Takeaways:
‚úî Define background tasks using '@shared_task' for flexibility and decoupling.  
‚úî Trigger tasks using '.delay()' to enqueue them for Celery workers.  
‚úî Celery workers must be restarted when new tasks are introduced.  
‚úî Redis ensures task delivery, even if Celery workers are temporarily offline.  
‚úî This pattern improves responsiveness and scalability by freeing the web server.




















Scheduling Periodic Tasks: 
--------------------------
üìå Scheduling Periodic Tasks with Celery Beat
In many applications, we need to run certain tasks on a schedule, such as:
- Generating periodic reports
- Sending batch emails
- Running maintenance routines (e.g., cleanup jobs)

To automate these tasks, we use Celery Beat, a periodic task scheduler that triggers Celery tasks at specific intervals.





üìå 1. What Is Celery Beat?
Celery Beat is a scheduler process that:
- Runs alongside your app and worker processes
- Kicks off tasks at scheduled intervals
- Delegates actual task execution to Celery workers

Analogy: Just as a heartbeat triggers blood flow at regular intervals, Celery Beat triggers tasks periodically while workers handle the actual processing.






üìå 2. Defining the Periodic Task Schedule:
üìÑ File: 'storefront/settings.py'
from celery.schedules import crontab

CELERY_BEAT_SCHEDULE = {
    'notify_customers': {
        'task': 'playground.tasks.notify_customers',
        'schedule': 5,  # Every 5 seconds (for demo purposes)
        'args': ['Hello World'],
    }
}

üîç Key Parameters:
| Setting         | Description                                                |
|-----------------|------------------------------------------------------------|
| 'task'          | Full import path to your Celery task function              |
| 'schedule'      | When to trigger the task (in seconds or using 'crontab')   |
| 'args'          | Positional arguments to pass to the task                   |
| 'kwargs'        | (Optional) Keyword arguments as a dictionary               |

‚úî Naming convention: Task key (''notify_customers'') can be anything, but using the function name improves clarity.







üìå 3. Customizing the Schedule with 'crontab()'

For more granular control (e.g., specific days or hours), use the 'crontab' object:
'schedule': crontab(day_of_week=1, hour=7, minute=30)  # Every Monday at 7:30 AM

Additional Examples:
crontab(minute='*/15')  # Every 15 minutes
crontab(hour=3)         # Every day at 3 AM
crontab(day_of_week='0', hour=23, minute=0)  # Every Sunday at 11 PM


üìå Reference: [Celery Beat Schedule Documentation](https://docs.celeryq.dev/en/stable/userguide/periodic-tasks.html)





üìå 4. Starting the Celery Beat Scheduler

In a new terminal window, run:
celery -A storefront beat

‚úî This starts the Beat scheduler process  
‚úî Beat connects to the message broker and begins triggering tasks as defined in 'CELERY_BEAT_SCHEDULE'





üìå 5. Verifying Task Execution
Open the Celery worker terminal window:
- You‚Äôll see 'notify_customers' firing every 5 seconds (or at your defined schedule).
- Each task is enqueued and processed by Celery workers in the background.




Codes and Other Notes in this Discussion: 

Periodic Tasks: 

Generating periodic reports 
Sending emails 
Running maintenance jobs 


Celery Beat 


storefront/settings.py:
from celery.schedules import crontab  

CELERY_BEAT_SCHEDULE = {
    'notify_customers': {
        'task': 'playground.tasks.notify_customers',
        'schedule': 5,   # or 'schedule': crontab(day_of_week=1, hour=7, minute=30),
        'args': ['Hello World'],
    }
}


celery -A storefront beat 




‚úÖ Summary

| Step | Action |
|------|--------|
| 1    | Define 'CELERY_BEAT_SCHEDULE' in 'settings.py' |
| 2    | Use raw seconds or 'crontab()' for scheduling |
| 3    | Pass arguments using 'args' or 'kwargs' |
| 4    | Start Celery Beat with 'celery -A storefront beat' |
| 5    | Observe task execution in the worker logs |



Using Celery + Beat + Redis gives you a reliable and extensible platform for background and scheduled task execution. 






















Monitoring Celery Tasks: 
------------------------
üìä Monitoring Celery Tasks with Flower:
Once you‚Äôve defined and executed tasks with Celery, it's important to monitor their status and worker activity. For that, we use Flower‚Äîa real-time web-based monitoring tool for Celery.



üìå 1. What is Flower?

Flower (pronounced *flow-er*) is a lightweight monitoring tool that provides a browser-based UI for:
- Tracking active, scheduled, and failed tasks
- Viewing task details and metadata
- Managing and inspecting Celery workers
- Viewing task queues and broker information


üìå 2. Installing Flower
Use Pipenv to install Flower in your Django environment:
pipenv install flower




üìå 3. Starting Flower
Run Flower as a separate process using the Celery CLI:
celery -A storefront flower

‚úî '-A storefront' tells Celery to look for the 'celery' app in your Django project.




üìå 4. Accessing the Flower Dashboard

Open your browser and navigate to:
http://localhost:5555

You‚Äôll see a real-time dashboard with several key sections.



üìå 5. Dashboard Features Overview
üîπ Workers Panel
- Shows list of connected workers
- Displays:
  - Uptime
  - Status (online/offline)
  - Number of active, reserved, and completed tasks

üîπ Tasks Panel
- Lists task execution history with metadata:
  - Task name
  - Unique task ID
  - Status: success, failed, received, etc.
  - Arguments and return value
  - Start and end timestamps

üîπ Broker Panel
- Shows broker type (e.g., Redis)
- Displays active queues and routing stats
- Confirms that Flower is successfully connected to the broker





üìå 6. Example Usage:
After triggering some Celery tasks (like 'notify_customers'), Flower will display:

- Real-time status updates
- Log outputs from those tasks
- Argument payloads (e.g., '["Hello World"]')
- Whether the task succeeded or failed

‚úî Makes it easy to troubleshoot and verify if background jobs are completing as expected.





Codes and Other Notes in this Discussion: 
Flower --> (flow-er)

pipenv install flower


celery -A storefront flower 


localhost:5555



‚úÖ Key Takeaways:
‚úî Flower provides real-time visibility into Celery task execution  
‚úî Install it with 'pipenv install flower' and run it via 'celery -A storefront flower'  
‚úî Access the UI at 'http://localhost:5555' to monitor workers, tasks, and brokers  
‚úî Ideal for diagnosing task failures, verifying job completion, and checking queue health



With Flower running alongside Celery, you now have complete transparency into your background task system. 





















Automated Testing:
------------------

Introduction: 
In this section, we'll be talking about automated testing. Now a lot of people find automated testing confusing, so I'm going to simplify it for you and give you a practical recipe you can follow to test your applications. Now, let's jump in and get started.



What is Automated Testing:
--------------------------
‚úÖ Introduction to Automated Testing
As your application grows in complexity, manual testing becomes increasingly time-consuming and error-prone. Automated testing solves this by allowing you to write code that programmatically verifies the behavior of your system.



üì¶ Why Testing Matters
During development, we implemented multiple API endpoints for:
- Collections
- Products
- Carts
- Orders

Each of these endpoints supports actions like:
- 'POST' (create)
- 'GET' (retrieve)
- 'PUT/PATCH' (update)
- 'DELETE' (remove)

However, many of these actions are governed by rules. For instance:
üîê Only admin users can modify products.  
‚ùå Anonymous users or regular users should be restricted.

Testing these rules manually in the browser is fine at first, but over time:
- The number of edge cases increases
- Test coverage becomes inconsistent
- Repeating tests slows down your workflow




ü§ñ What Is Automated Testing?
Automated testing is the practice of:
1. Writing test scripts (just like code)
2. Running them to simulate interactions with your app
3. Verifying that the actual behavior matches expected outcomes

This allows you to:
- Catch regressions quickly
- Save time with repeatable, fast tests
- Release updates confidently

You write the test once, and run it as often as needed.




üß† Hidden Rules + Forgotten Logic
Over time, you'll forget the small rules and assumptions built into each endpoint unless:
- You document them rigorously (which rarely happens)
- Or automate them in tests

‚ö†Ô∏è Without tests, even small changes can unexpectedly break features you didn‚Äôt think were related.



‚öîÔ∏è The Double-Edged Sword of Automated Testing:
Automated testing is extremely powerful when used correctly:
‚úîÔ∏è It ensures long-term stability  
‚úîÔ∏è It prevents careless regressions  
‚úîÔ∏è It boosts confidence in your codebase

But when done poorly:
‚ùå It creates brittle, hard-to-maintain test cases  
‚ùå It slows down development  
‚ùå It leads to frustration and test fatigue




üí¨ A Common Misconception:
You might hear people like the mythical ‚ÄúJohn Smith‚Äù say:
"Automated testing is a waste of time."

And sure, it can feel that way‚Äîespecially if:
- You‚Äôre testing the wrong things
- You write fragile tests
- You follow outdated practices

Even seasoned developers fall into the trap of writing ‚Äúuseless‚Äù tests. But with the right strategies and principles, tests become tools‚Äînot obstacles.



‚úÖ Key Takeaways
- Manually testing APIs is not scalable.
- Automated tests are written once and run repeatedly.
- They improve speed, reliability, and developer confidence.
- If poorly written, they can become a burden.
- Understanding the *right* way to test is essential (and covered in upcoming lessons).



















Test Behaviours, Not Implementations:
-------------------------------------
‚úÖ Test Behaviors, Not Implementations
A common pitfall in automated testing is focusing on how the code is implemented, rather than how it behaves from an external perspective. This section explains why behavior-based testing is essential for writing resilient and maintainable tests.



üéØ What Does It Mean to Test Behavior?
Imagine testing a microwave:

- You press the Start button.
- You observe the monitor to see if the timer counts down from one minute.

You're verifying whether the microwave behaves correctly‚Äînot whether each transistor inside is firing properly.  
‚úÖ You're testing the outcome, not the internal mechanics.



üß™ How This Applies to Software Testing
When testing a web application (e.g., a Django API), people often write tests for the internal components:
- Models
- Views
- Serializers
- Routers

But these components may evolve:
| Change                                 | Example                              |
|----------------------------------------|--------------------------------------|
| Function-based view ‚Üí class-based view | You restructure for readability      |
| One model becomes two models           | You normalize your database          |
| API routing is revised                 | You switch from 'urls.py' to routers |

üõë If your tests are too closely tied to these implementation details, they‚Äôll break with every refactor‚Äîeven if the behavior remains the same.





‚úÖ What Should We Test?
Focus on external behaviors:
- What requests are allowed?
- What responses are returned?
- What side effects (e.g., database changes, emails) should occur?





üí° Practical Example: Testing Collection Creation
We want to verify how the '/collections/' POST endpoint behaves, not how it‚Äôs implemented.

Behavior Scenarios:
1. Unauthorized Access
   - Request: Client is *not authenticated*
   - Expected Response: '401 Unauthorized'

2. Non-admin Access
   - Request: Client is authenticated, but not an admin
   - Expected Response: '403 Forbidden'

3. Missing Required Data
   - Request: Admin user submits request without a 'name'
   - Expected Response: '400 Bad Request'
   - Response Body: Should include an error for 'name' field

4. Valid Request
   - Request: Admin user submits a valid 'name'
   - Expected Response: '201 Created' (or '200 OK')
   - Response Body: Should include the new collection‚Äôs ID

These tests should simulate real API requests and validate expected responses‚Äîregardless of how views or models are structured internally.



‚ö†Ô∏è Why Testing Implementation Can Be Risky
- Increases test brittleness
- Slows down refactoring
- Leads to redundant or low-value tests
- Obscures your app‚Äôs actual business logic and behavior

‚ÄúTest the *what*, not the *how*.‚Äù



‚úÖ Key Takeaways
‚úî Write tests that validate what the system does, not how it‚Äôs built  
‚úî Focus on the API contract: inputs, outputs, and side effects  
‚úî Avoid coupling tests to views, serializers, or database schemas  
‚úî Well-written tests should survive refactors as long as behavior remains intact











Tooling:
--------
üì¶ Tooling: Choosing a Test Framework in Django

Just as Django gives us a framework for building APIs, we also need a framework for writing and running tests. A good test framework provides:

- A structure to define tests
- A test runner to execute them
- Clear reports showing which tests passed or failed



üß™ Python Testing Frameworks

Two of the most popular options for testing Python applications are:

| Framework     | Description                                                |
|---------------|------------------------------------------------------------|
| 'unittest'    | Built into Python, inspired by Java‚Äôs JUnit                |
| 'pytest'      | Third-party tool with more features, plugins, and less boilerplate |



ü§è Why Choose Pytest?
‚úî Cleaner, shorter test syntax  
‚úî Huge ecosystem and active community  
‚úî Tons of plugins for advanced workflows (e.g. test coverage, fixtures, mocking)  
‚úî Easier to scale across large projects

Here‚Äôs a comparison:
# Using unittest
class TestAddition(unittest.TestCase):
    def test_sum(self):
        self.assertEqual(1 + 1, 2)

# Using pytest
def test_sum():
    assert 1 + 1 == 2


üéØ Pytest is clearly more concise and beginner-friendly.




üß∞ Installing Pytest for Django Projects
Since tests are only used during development, we install Pytest as a development dependency:
pipenv install --dev pytest

‚úî Adds 'pytest' under '[dev-packages]' in your 'Pipfile'  
‚úî Keeps production deployments clean and lightweight

You‚Äôll see something like:

'''toml
[dev-packages]
pytest = "*"
'''



üîå Django Integration: pytest-django
To test Django apps more effectively with Pytest, install the official integration plugin:
pipenv install --dev pytest-django

‚úî Enables database testing, Django settings access, fixtures like 'client', and more  
‚úî Compatible with Pytest syntax and Django testing patterns




Codes and Other Notes in this Discussion: 
Test Frameworks:
unittest
pytest - More Features, Tons of plugins, Less boilerplate 


pipenv install --dev pytest 

pipenv install --dev pytest-django 




‚úÖ Summary

| Tool             | Purpose                               |
|------------------|---------------------------------------|
| 'pytest'         | Core test framework with clean syntax |
| 'pytest-django'  | Pytest plugin for Django projects     |


















Your First Test:
----------------
‚úÖ Writing Your First Automated Test with Pytest & Django REST Framework
Let‚Äôs walk through building a clean and maintainable test using Pytest and Django REST Framework. This serves as the foundation for testing API behavior in your Django projects.


üìÅ 1. Organize Test Files Properly

In your app directory (e.g. 'store/'):

- Create a folder named 'tests/'  
  üî∏ Important: Must be plural ('tests') ‚Äî Pytest looks for this naming convention by default.

- Inside that, create a test file:  
  üìÑ 'test_collections.py'  
  üî∏ Test file names must start with 'test_' to be picked up by Pytest.





üß™ 2. Structure of a Test

Each test should follow the AAA structure:

| Stage   | Meaning                      | Description                                                       |
|---------|------------------------------|-------------------------------------------------------------------|
| Arrange | Set up test state            | Create objects, define data, prepare environment                  |
| Act     | Perform the action           | Trigger API call or code you want to test                         |
| Assert  | Verify outcome               | Check status codes, responses, database changes, etc.             |





üí° 3. Naming Conventions Matter

Test discovery depends on naming. Follow these rules:
- Functions: start with 'test_'
- Classes (optional): start with 'Test' (e.g. 'TestCreateCollection')
- Use descriptive function names (e.g. 'test_if_user_is_anonymous_returns_401')

‚úîÔ∏è Clear naming ensures your tests communicate what behavior is being validated.





üßæ 4. Sample Test Implementation

üìÑ File: 'store/tests/test_collections.py'

from rest_framework.test import APIClient
from rest_framework import status

class TestCreateCollection:
    def test_if_user_is_anonymous_returns_401(self):
        client = APIClient()
        response = client.post('/store/collections/', {'title': 'a'})

        assert response.status_code == status.HTTP_401_UNAUTHORIZED


üß† Notes:
- No need to over-name collections ‚Äî ''a'' is sufficient in tests to avoid noise
- The endpoint must end with a trailing slash ('/') unless you‚Äôve changed Django‚Äôs default router settings
- The test simulates a POST request with an anonymous user (not authenticated)
- Expected result: '401 Unauthorized'



üîé Behavior Under Test

| Scenario                                        | Expected Result                |
|-------------------------------------------------|--------------------------------|
| Anonymous user tries to create a collection     | ‚ùå Request denied with '401'    |
| Authenticated non-admin ‚Üí '403' (tested later)  |
| Missing name ‚Üí '400'                            |
| Valid admin & payload ‚Üí '201 Created'           |





Codes and other Notes in this Discussion: 

store/tests/test_collections.py:

from rest_framework.test import APIClient 
from rest_framework import status

class TestCreateCollection: 
	def test_if_user_is_anonymous_returns_401(self): 
	# Arrange
	
	
	# Act
	client = APIClient()
	response = client.post('store/collections/', {'title': 'a'})
	
	# Assert
	assert response.status_code == status.HTTP_401_UNAUTHORIZED
	
	
	
	

‚úÖ Key Takeaways
‚úî Follow naming conventions strictly for Pytest to auto-discover tests  
‚úî Organize tests based on use case (e.g. collection creation vs deletion)  
‚úî Use clear, behavior-focused function names ‚Äî avoid vague names like 'test_create'  
‚úî Stick to the Arrange ‚Üí Act ‚Üí Assert structure  
‚úî Validate API behavior, not implementation details















Running Tests:
--------------
‚úÖ Running Tests in Django with Pytest
Once your tests are written, it's time to run them and verify that your application behaves as expected. This note outlines how to configure, validate, and selectively run tests using Pytest in a Django project.



üìÅ 1. Configuring Pytest for Django
Pytest needs to know which Django settings module to use. You do this by creating a configuration file at the project root:

üìÑ File: 'pytest.ini'
[pytest]
DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE=storefront.settings

‚úî This allows you to run 'pytest' without manually exporting environment variables.



üß™ 2. Running All Tests
From your terminal, run:
pytest

You should see an output like:
collected 1 item
.                          [100%]
1 passed in 0.30s




‚ùó 3. Ensuring Tests Are Reliable (No "Lying Tests")
Even if a test passes, it may not be testing anything meaningful. To verify integrity:
- Comment out or remove the logic in your views that the test expects
- Run the test again
- It should fail if the test is written correctly

If it still passes ‚Äî it's a "lying test" ‚úÖ‚ö†Ô∏è


Example: Testing Permissions in a View
You remove a permission class from your view, and rerun the test. Now it should raise:
AssertionError: assert 201 == 401


‚úî This confirms the test was correctly detecting unauthorized behavior.




üìå 4. Fixing "Database Access Not Allowed" Error:
Pytest restricts database access by default. To allow test functions to use the DB, mark them with the '@pytest.mark.django_db' decorator.

Instead of adding it to each test:
@pytest.mark.django_db
class TestCreateCollection:
    def test_if_user_is_anonymous_returns_401(self):
        ...

‚úî This applies the marker to all test methods inside the class.




‚úÖ 5. Summary Test Example
üìÑ 'store/tests/test_collections.py':

import pytest
from rest_framework.test import APIClient
from rest_framework import status

@pytest.mark.django_db
class TestCreateCollection:
    def test_if_user_is_anonymous_returns_401(self):
        client = APIClient()
        response = client.post('/store/collections/', {'title': 'a'})
        assert response.status_code == status.HTTP_401_UNAUTHORIZED




üîç 6. Selectively Running Tests

When dealing with large test suites, it's helpful to run a targeted subset:

'pytest store/tests' = Run all tests in the 'store/tests' folder 
'pytest store/tests/test_collections.py' = Run tests in a single module 
'pytest store/tests/test_collections.py::TestCreateCollection'  = Run all tests in a class 
'pytest store/tests/test_collections.py::TestCreateCollection::test_if_user_is_anonymous_returns_401' =  Run a specific test method 
'pytest -k anonymous' = Run any tests with "anonymous" in their name 




Codes and Other Notes in this Discussion: 

pytest.ini:

[pytest]
DJANGO_SETTINGS_MODULE=storefront.settings


in the terminal:
pytest 


store/tests/test_collections.py:
from rest_framework.test import APIClient 
from rest_framework import status
import pytest 

@pytest.mark.django_db 
class TestCreateCollection: 
	
	def test_if_user_is_anonymous_returns_401(self): 
	# Arrange
	
	
	# Act
	client = APIClient()
	response = client.post('store/collections/', {'title': 'a'})
	
	# Assert
	assert response.status_code == status.HTTP_401_UNAUTHORIZED
	
	
	
different ways of running pytest:	
pytest store/tests
pytest store/tests/test_collections.py::TestCreateCollection::test_if_user_is_anonymous_returns_401
pytest -k anonymous 





üéØ Key Takeaways

‚úî Use a 'pytest.ini' file to configure settings  
‚úî Decorate tests or classes with '@pytest.mark.django_db' to allow DB access  
‚úî Always verify that tests fail when the expected logic is removed  
‚úî Use 'pytest -k' and scoped test paths to run selected tests efficiently  
‚úî Well-written tests check behavior, not implementation













Skipping Tests:
---------------
‚úÖ Skipping Tests Temporarily in Pytest
During development, you may encounter a failing test that you're not ready to fix‚Äîperhaps because you‚Äôre focused on another task or feature. Rather than leaving the test in a failing state (which can cause noise or distract you), you can temporarily skip it using Pytest‚Äôs built-in tools.



üìå Why Skip Tests?
- You‚Äôre in the middle of another feature.
- Fixing the failure requires significant refactoring.
- You want to prevent unnecessary test noise while working.

‚úî Skipping the test hides the failure temporarily while keeping the test code intact.  
‚úî It allows the rest of your test suite to run cleanly.



üß™ Applying the Skip Decorator
To skip a test, use '@pytest.mark.skip' as a decorator.

üìÑ Example:
import pytest
from rest_framework.test import APIClient
from rest_framework import status

@pytest.mark.django_db
class TestCreateCollection:
    
    @pytest.mark.skip
    def test_if_user_is_anonymous_returns_401(self):
        client = APIClient()
        response = client.post('/store/collections/', {'title': 'a'})

        assert response.status_code == status.HTTP_401_UNAUTHORIZED


‚úî This tells Pytest to skip this test method when the suite is executed.

üìü Running Pytest After Skipping
pytest

You‚Äôll see output like:
collected 1 item
s                          [100%]
==================== 1 skipped in 0.05s =====================

‚úî The 's' stands for ‚Äúskipped‚Äù  
‚úî Pytest does not raise any failure alerts, allowing you to stay focused.



‚úÖ When You're Ready...
Once you're ready to revisit the skipped test:
- Simply remove the '@pytest.mark.skip' decorator.
- Rerun the test to confirm it‚Äôs fixed and passing.




Codes and Other Notes in this Discussion:

@pytest.mark.django_db 
class TestCreateCollection: 
	@pytest.mark.skip
	def test_if_user_is_anonymous_returns_401(self): 
	client = APIClient()
	response = client.post('store/collections/', {'title': 'a'})
	
	assert response.status_code == status.HTTP_401_UNAUTHORIZED
	
	

üß† Key Takeaways

| Action                        | Description                                      |
|-------------------------------|--------------------------------------------------|
| '@pytest.mark.skip'           | Skips a test without removing it from the file  |
| Ideal for                     | Temporary suppression of failing or in-progress tests |
| Skipped tests result          | Tests marked with an 's' in the Pytest report   |
| Best practice                 | Add a comment or TODO so you remember to revisit |


	








Continuous Testing:
-------------------
‚úÖ Continuous Testing with Pytest-Watch
Running tests continuously during development allows you to catch issues the moment they‚Äôre introduced. This practice, known as continuous testing, provides instant feedback and improves code quality.


üîÑ Two Ways to Run Tests

| Method              | Description                                           |
|---------------------|-------------------------------------------------------|
| On Demand           | Run manually before commits, merges, or deployments   |
| Continuous Testing  | Tests re-run automatically as you edit files      |

While some developers worry that continuous testing might slow down machines (especially with large test suites), many others‚Äîespecially those using efficient tools like 'pytest'‚Äîfind it incredibly helpful.



üöÄ Setting Up Continuous Testing:
üîß Step 1: Install 'pytest-watch'

This Pytest plugin watches your code and test files for changes and re-runs tests automatically.
pipenv install --dev pytest-watch

‚úî This adds 'pytest-watch' to your '[dev-packages]' in 'Pipfile'.


üîß Step 2: Run Pytest Watcher
ptw

- Short for PyTestWatch
- Starts a persistent watcher in your terminal
- Re-runs tests every time a file changes

You can dedicate a separate terminal or monitor to keep 'ptw' running during development.




üß™ Live Feedback Example:
1. Edit a file under 'store/tests/'
2. Save your changes
3. ‚úÖ 'ptw' automatically detects the change and re-runs all tests

This lets you immediately see:
- Which test failed
- Why it failed
- If your recent change introduced a bug

üí° Similarly, editing your application code (e.g. 'views.py') triggers the same behavior.



üîÅ Example Workflow:
[Code Editor] -->     [App / View / Serializer]
              <--     
      ‚îÇ
      ‚ñº
[Test Change Detected] ‚Üí [ptw] re-runs tests ‚Üí ‚úÖ or ‚ùå feedback instantly




üß† Best Practices:
- Use 'ptw' while developing new features or debugging test failures
- Turn off continuous testing on resource-constrained systems if needed
- Always run full test suite ('pytest') before:
  - Committing changes
  - Merging pull requests
  - Deploying code




Codes and Other Notes in this Discussion: 

pipenv install --dev pytest-watch
